بشيء لا يستلزم العلم البديهي ببداهته ولذا استدل عليها وأما ما يقال أن ماهيته إذا حصلت للنفس بلا كسب والتفتت النفس إليها عرفت بمجرد التفاتها إليها أنها بغير كسب فتكون بداهة كل بديهي غنية عن الاحتجاج عليها، وكذا كسبية كل كسبي فجوابه أنه قد يحصل في النفس صورة، ولا يلتفت إلى كيفية حصولها، فإذا تطاولت المدة، وتكثرت الصور وتوجهت النفس إليها، فربما التبست عليها كيفية حصول بعضها، فاحتاجت إلى الاستدلال على أن الوجهين جاز أن يكونا تنبيهين. (والجواب ) عنه ( أن الضروري حصول علم جزئي ( متعلق بوجوده ) فإن هذا العلم حاصل لكل أحد بلا نظر (وهو) أي حصول ذلك العلم الجزئي ( غير تصوره وغير مستلزم (له إذ كثيراً ما تحصل لنا علوم جزئية بمعلومات مخصوصة، ولا نتصور تلك العلوم مع كونها حاصلة لنا، بل نحتاج في تصورها إلى توجه مستأنف إليها، فلا يكون حصولها عين تصورها ولا مستلزماً له ، وإذا لم يكن ذلك العلم الجزئي المتعلق بوجوده متصوراً ( فلا يلزم تصور العلم المطلق أصلاً ( فضلاً عن أن يكون ) تصوره ( ضرورياً)، ويجوز أن يجاب عنه أن يجاب عنه أيضاً بأنه إنما يتم إذا كان العلم ذاتياً لما تحته، وكان شيء من أفراده متصوراً بالكنه بديهةً وكلاهما ممنوعان ( لا يقال :) لا نقتصر على ما ذكر بل نقول : إن كل واحد يعلم بالضرورة أنه موجود، ( ويعلم ) أيضاً كذلك ( أنه يعلم بذلك والعلم أحد تصوري هذا التصديق)، وهو أيضاً، فيكون تصوره السابق على التصديق البديهي أولى أن يكون بديهياًنحن بديهي ( : قوله : فلا يكون حصولها عين (تصورها وما قيل من أن العلم بالعلم بعد الالتفات إليه ضروري على ما نص المصنف عليه في الأعراض، فيكون العلم بهذا العلم ضرورياً، والعلم المطلق جزء منه، فيتم التقريب فليس بشيء لأن معنى ذلك أن العلم بحصول العلم بعد الالتفات ضروري لا أن تصوره ضروري حتى يلزم ضرورية المطلق. لأنه قوله : ( والعلم أحد تصوري هذا التصديق) إنما قال : أحد التصورين من غير تعيين يجوز أن يجعل موضوعاً بأن يقال : العلم بهذا العلم ضروري، وأن يجعل محمولاً بأن يقال : كل أحد عالم بهذا العلم بالضرورة، وبهذا ظهر أنه لا يجوز أن يقال أحد التصورات الثلاثة، فمن قال : إنما لم يقل أحد التصورات اتباعاً للمتقدمين، وإن اعتبار تصور النسبة في التصديق من تدقيقات المتأخرين، فقد ركب شططاً . قوله : ( والجواب عنه أن الضروري حصول علم إلخ ) فإن قلت : سيجيء في بحث العلم من الإلهيات أن العلم بالعلم لا يتوقف إلا على الالتفات ولهذا ظن أن العلم بالشيء عين العلم بالعلم، وحينئذ يندفع هذا الجواب، ويحتاج إلى جواب الشارح قلت : المذكور فيما سيأتي أن من علم شيئاً أمكنه أن يعلم أنه عالم به، لا أن أنه يعلمه وإليه أشار في مباحث العلم من موقف الأعراض فجواب الشارح أظهر. قوله : ( والعلم أحد تصوري هذا التصديق إنما لم يقل أحد تصوراته اتباعاً للمتقدمين فإن اعتبار تصور النسبة في التصديق من تدقيقات المتأخرين .
The second observatory - on the definition of absolute knowledge. The first section on knowledge and its divisions, the second on speculation (al-naẓar) and what pertains to it, the third on the methods leading to theoretical objectives. (And in it), i.e., concerning absolute knowledge, (there are three doctrines). The (first) doctrine (is that it is necessary/immediate (ḍarūrī)), i.e., the conceptualization of its quiddity (māhiyya) in its essence. And Imam al-Razi chose it for two reasons. The (first reason) (is that every person's knowledge of his existence), i.e., that he exists, (is necessary), i.e., obtained without acquisition and speculation. (And this is particular knowledge) belonging to a particular object which is his existence. (And absolute knowledge is a part of it) because the absolute is essential to the restricted. (And knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole). So when the particular knowledge, which is a whole for everyone, is obtained necessarily, then absolute knowledge, which is its part, precedes it. (And what precedes the necessary is more worthy of being necessary, so absolute knowledge is necessary). His saying: (for two reasons), i.e., two proofs, based on the fact that the judgment of the self-evidence of the self-evident may be theoretical in the manner of its initial occurrence due to the lack of practice in its occurrence and its intermingling with many knowledges, or two reminders based on the fact that the judgment of self-evidence is also self-evident, but the frequency of discussion about them results from the fact that they are [due to] heedlessness of two reminders. Self-evident. His saying: (i.e., that he exists, etc.) He did not interpret it as apparent, namely that everyone's conceptualization of his existence, because the Imam established in his books that it is knowledge that he exists, not because it is objected against him that if what is meant is particular existence, we do not concede that its conceptualization is self-evident, and if what is meant is existence restricted by addition, then it is a branch of the affirmation of absolute existence, and we do not concede its affirmation. And because there is discussion about the self-evidence of its conceptualization whether particular existence is meant or restricted, since the majority of theologians (mutakallimūn) denied particular existence and affirmed individuation (al-khuṣūṣ), and the Shaykh denied individuation due to his denial of absolute existence. Then it is not hidden that the particular necessary sciences from conceptualizations and assents are many, so specifying the inference by the self-evidence of this particular science, namely the knowledge of his existence, based on the fact that it is the most prior of the necessary sciences, according to what they said: The direction of the first proof according to the law of inference is that it be said: Absolute knowledge precedes necessary knowledge, and what precedes the necessary is necessary. As for the major premise, it is apparent. As for the minor, because it is a part of the necessary knowledge that he exists, and the part precedes the whole. As for its being a part of it, because it is absolute and that is restricted, and the absolute is part of the restricted. As for its necessity, because it is obtained without acquisition, and everything whose state is this is necessary. It is disputed and doubted that it is derived from that; otherwise it would be an enigma avoided in this context, not beneficial for implication, as is not hidden. His saying: (for two reasons, the first that every person's knowledge, etc.) The self-evidence of knowledge of something does not entail self-evident knowledge of its self-evidence; therefore it is inferred. As for what is said that if its quiddity occurs to the soul without acquisition and the soul attends to it, it knows merely by its attending to it that it is without acquisition, so the self-evidence of every self-evident is independent of proof, and likewise the acquiredness of every acquired, then the answer is that an image may occur in the soul without attending to how it occurred. When time passes and images multiply and the soul turns to them, perhaps the manner of occurrence of some is confused, so it needs inference. So the two reasons could be two reminders. (And the answer) to it (is that the necessary is the occurrence of particular knowledge) related to his existence; for this knowledge is obtained by everyone without speculation. (And it), i.e., the occurrence of that particular knowledge, (is not its conceptualization nor does it entail it) because often particular knowledges occur to us concerning specific objects, and we do not conceptualize those knowledges despite their occurring to us; rather, we need a renewed attention to conceptualize them. So their occurrence is neither identical to their conceptualization nor entailing it. And if that particular knowledge related to his existence is not conceptualized, (then the conceptualization of absolute knowledge does not follow at all), let alone that its conceptualization be necessary. And it may be answered about it also that it is only complete if knowledge is essential to what is under it and if some of its individuals are conceptually grasped by essence self-evidently, and both are denied. (It should not be said:) We do not restrict ourselves to what was mentioned; rather we say: Everyone knows necessarily that he exists, and also knows similarly that he knows that, and knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations of this assent, and it is also [immediate], so its conceptualization preceding the self-evident assent is more worthy of being self-evident. We [say] self-evident: His saying: so its occurrence is not identical to its conceptualization. And what is said that knowledge of knowledge after attention is necessary according to what the author stated in [the chapter on] accidents, so knowledge of this knowledge is necessary, and absolute knowledge is part of it, so the approximation is complete, it is nothing because the meaning of that is that knowledge of the occurrence of knowledge after attention is necessary, not that its conceptualization is necessary such that the necessity of the absolute follows. Because his saying: (and knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations of this assent) He only said one of the two conceptualizations without specification; it may be made subject by saying knowledge of this knowledge is necessary, or predicate by saying everyone knows this knowledge necessarily. And by this it appears that it is not permissible to say one of the three conceptualizations. Whoever says that he only did not say one of the conceptualizations following the predecessors, and that considering the conceptualization of the relation in the assent is from the refinements of the later scholars, has certainly committed excess. His saying: (And the answer to it that the necessary is the occurrence of knowledge, etc.) For if you say: It will come in the discussion of knowledge from the divine matters that knowledge of knowledge depends only on attention; therefore it is thought that knowledge of a thing is identical to knowledge of knowledge. Then this answer is refuted, and the commentator's answer is needed. I say: What is mentioned in what will come is that whoever knows a thing can know that he knows it, not that knowledge depends only on attention. Moreover, the opponent's doubt is about the affirmation of God's knowledge, so we are not obliged to concede it. The truth is that what is mentioned in the divine matters, even if it is as mentioned, yet the apparent is that whoever knows a thing and attends to it knows merely by his attention that he knows it. And to this he pointed in the discussions of knowledge from the Station of Accidents, so the commentator's answer is more evident. His saying: (And knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations of this assent) He only did not say one of its three conceptualizations following the predecessors, for considering the conceptualization of the relation in the assent is among the refinements of the later scholars.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument about the definition of absolute knowledge (al-ʿilm al-muṭlaq). The author first presents the first of three opinions: that the conceptualization of absolute knowledge is necessary/immediate (ḍarūrī), meaning it is acquired without effort or speculation. This opinion is attributed to Imam al-Razi, who offers two proofs. The first proof is explained in detail: everyone has immediate knowledge of their own existence; this is a particular instance of knowledge (ʿilm khāṣṣ); absolute knowledge is a part of it (since the absolute is a genus for the restricted); knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole; therefore, since the particular knowledge is necessary, the absolute knowledge that is its part must be even more prior and thus also necessary.

The commentary then elaborates on this proof, noting potential objections and clarifications. For example, it discusses whether the knowledge of existence is really self-evident and addresses the possibility that the proof could be seen as a reminder rather than a strict demonstration.

Next, the passage presents a response (al-jawāb) to al-Razi's proof. The response distinguishes between the mere occurrence (ḥuṣūl) of particular knowledge and its conceptualization (taṣawwur). It argues that while the knowledge of existence occurs to everyone without speculation, this occurrence does not entail that one has conceptualized that knowledge. Often we have particular knowledges without being aware of them as concepts; we need to attend to them separately to conceptualize them. Therefore, the occurrence of particular knowledge does not imply the conceptualization of absolute knowledge, let alone its necessity.

Then a further objection (lā yuqāl) is raised: everyone not only knows they exist but also knows that they know this. This second-order knowledge (that one knows) is an immediate assent (taṣdīq), and knowledge is one of its two conceptualizations. Since the conceptualization of knowledge precedes the assent, it should be even more immediate. The response to this objection clarifies that the necessary knowledge of knowledge after attention is only the judgment that one knows, not the conceptualization of the essence of knowledge. The conceptualization itself may still be acquired. Further commentary discusses the distinction between the subject and predicate in the judgment and refutes the idea that three conceptualizations are involved, following the method of the later scholars.

Finally, additional commentary responds to a potential objection from the discussion of knowledge in the divine attributes, asserting that the later explanation of knowledge of knowledge requiring only attention does not undermine the present argument, as the issue there is different.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That having a particular knowledge automatically means one has conceptualized that knowledge.
Correction: The response clarifies that occurrence is not conceptualization; one can have knowledge without forming a concept of it.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does the necessary occurrence of particular knowledge of existence not prove that absolute knowledge is necessary?
A) Because absolute knowledge is not part of that particular knowledge.
B) Because the occurrence of knowledge is not the same as its conceptualization.
C) Because the particular knowledge is not necessary.
D) Because the proof is only for the second opinion.

Key Takeaway
The critical response shows that necessary occurrence does not imply necessary conceptualization, thus blocking al-Razi's proof for the necessity of absolute knowledge.

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Knowledge of Existence Does Not Imply Conceptualization of Absolute Knowledge
Author presents an objection and two responses about knowledge and conceptualization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of knowing one's own existence does not necessitate conceptualizing absolute knowledge, and the two responses to this objection.

Core Idea
Knowing something is not the same as conceptualizing that knowledge.

This passage addresses an objection: if knowledge of one's own existence is necessary, then absolute knowledge (the universal) must also be necessary because the particular falls under the universal. The author provides two responses. First, the occurrence of particular knowledge is not its conceptualization; we often have knowledge without being aware of its nature. Second, the entailment would only hold if knowledge were essential to the particular and if some particular were intuitively known by essence—both denied.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening line of the passage which states the objection: that necessary knowledge of existence entails absolute knowledge. Note how the objector reasons from particular to universal.
The objection is implicit in the phrase 'المطلوب والجواب عنه أن الضروري حصول علم جزئي'.
2
Study the first response
Focus on the phrase 'وهو غير تصوره وغير مستلزم له'. This is the core of the first response: the occurrence of knowledge is not its conceptualization and does not necessitate it. The author gives an example: we often have particular knowledges without conceptualizing them.
The distinction between having knowledge and conceptualizing it is crucial.
3
Study the second response
The second response begins with 'ويجوز أن يجاب عنه أيضاً'. It argues that the objection's entailment only works if knowledge is essential (dhātī) to what is under it and if some individual is intuitively known by essence (bi-al-kunn badīhatan). Both are denied.
This response tackles the premises of the objection rather than the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلوب (والجواب ) عنه ( أن الضروري حصول علم جزئي ( متعلق بوجوده ) فإن هذا العلم حاصل لكل أحد بلا نظر (وهو) أي حصول ذلك العلم الجزئي ( غير تصوره وغير مستلزم (له إذ كثيراً ما تحصل لنا علوم جزئية بمعلومات مخصوصة، ولا نتصور تلك العلوم مع كونها حاصلة لنا، بل نحتاج في تصورها إلى توجه مستأنف إليها، فلا يكون حصولها عين تصورها ولا مستلزماً له ، وإذا لم يكن ذلك العلم الجزئي المتعلق بوجوده متصوراً ( فلا يلزم تصور العلم المطلق أصلاً ( فضلاً عن أن يكون ) تصوره ( ضرورياً)، ويجوز أن يجاب عنه أن يجاب عنه أيضاً بأنه إنما يتم إذا كان العلم ذاتياً لما تحته، وكان شيء من أفراده متصوراً بالكنه بديهةً وكلاهما ممنوعان
The desired [objection] and the answer to it: that the necessary thing is the occurrence of a particular knowledge (ʿilm juzʾī) related to His existence, for this knowledge is obtained by everyone without reflection (naẓar). And it—i.e., the occurrence of that particular knowledge—is not its conceptualization (taṣawwur) nor does it necessitate it, for often particular knowledges of specific known things occur to us, but we do not conceptualize those knowledges while they are present to us; rather, we need in their conceptualization a renewed attention to them. So their occurrence is neither identical to their conceptualization nor does it necessitate it. And when that particular knowledge related to His existence is not conceptualized, then conceptualization of absolute knowledge (al-ʿilm al-muṭlaq) is not entailed at all, let alone that its conceptualization be necessary. And it is also possible to answer it [the objection] by saying that it [the argument] only holds if knowledge is essential (dhātī) to what is under it, and if some of its individuals are conceptualized by essence (bi-al-kunn) intuitively (badīhatan)—both of which are denied.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and two responses. The objector claims that since knowledge of one's existence is necessary, absolute knowledge must also be necessary because the particular is under the universal. The first response argues that the occurrence of that particular knowledge is not its conceptualization; we often have knowledge without being aware of its nature, so no conceptualization of the particular occurs, and thus no conceptualization of the universal is entailed. The second response argues that the entailment would only hold if the universal were essential to the particular and if some particular were intuitively known by essence—both conditions are denied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Having knowledge of something automatically means conceptualizing that knowledge (being aware of its nature).
Correction: The passage clarifies that the occurrence of knowledge (ḥuṣūl) is distinct from its conceptualization (taṣawwur). We often have knowledge without conceptualizing it; conceptualization requires renewed attention.

Checkpoint
According to the first response, why does the necessity of particular knowledge not entail the necessity of absolute knowledge?
A) Because absolute knowledge is impossible to conceptualize.
B) Because the occurrence of particular knowledge is not its conceptualization, and conceptualization is required for entailment.
C) Because knowledge of existence is only obtained through reflection.
D) Because the objector's argument is invalid in all cases.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of particular knowledge of existence does not entail conceptualization of absolute knowledge because occurrence and conceptualization are distinct, and the required conditions of essentiality and intuitive essence are not met.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have knowledge of Allah's existence, but have you ever actually sat down to conceptualize that knowledge? When was the last time you paused to reflect on what it means that He exists, not just that you 'know' it? What will you do today to move from mere occurrence to genuine conceptualization?
?
The passage says we need 'renewed attention' to conceptualize even our own knowledge. Think of one Islamic belief you hold but have not thoughtfully examined. Will you take 10 minutes today to bring it to the forefront of your mind and deliberately conceptualize it?
?
You often rely on 'necessary' or 'obvious' truths in your prayer and daily life. But this lesson warns that necessity does not guarantee conceptualization. Are there any aspects of your faith that you have left as 'automatic' knowledge without ever truly contemplating? How will you change that starting this week?

Knowledge as a Component of Assent: Objection and Refutation
Objection that knowledge is a component of the assent, and answer refutes the necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that knowledge is a component of a necessary assent fails to prove that its conceptualization is necessary, and why the distinction between occurrence and conceptualization is crucial.

Core Idea
Necessary occurrence of knowledge does not mean necessary conceptualization.

Having established the framework of assent and its components, an objector pushes further: if everyone knows their own existence and knows that they know it, then knowledge itself becomes part of that assent—so its conceptualization should be intuitive. This challenge requires careful unpacking. The answer clarifies a subtle but vital distinction between the occurrence of knowledge and its conceptualization, showing that the objection rests on a false equivalence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims that since everyone knows that they exist, they also know that they know—so knowledge is a component of the assent. This would make the conceptualization of knowledge prior to the assent, and thus itself intuitive.
Notice that the objector is trying to force the conceptualization of absolute knowledge to be intuitive.
2
Identify the key distinction in the answer
The answer first points out that occurrence of knowledge is not the same as its conceptualization. Then it shows that the phrase 'knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations' is ambiguous—it could refer to knowledge as subject or predicate, so it cannot force necessity of absolute knowledge's conceptualization.
This distinction between occurrence and conceptualization is crucial.
3
Reflect on why the objection fails
Even if knowledge of knowledge is necessary after attention, that necessity applies to the occurrence, not to the conceptualization. The objection conflates these two, and the ambiguity of 'one of the two' prevents any forced conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال :) لا نقتصر على ما ذكر بل نقول : إن كل واحد يعلم بالضرورة أنه موجود، ( ويعلم ) أيضاً كذلك ( أنه يعلم بذلك والعلم أحد تصوري هذا التصديق)، وهو أيضاً، فيكون تصوره السابق على التصديق البديهي أولى أن يكون بديهياً نحن بديهي ( : قوله : فلا يكون حصولها عين (تصورها وما قيل من أن العلم بالعلم بعد الالتفات إليه ضروري على ما نص المصنف عليه في الأعراض، فيكون العلم بهذا العلم ضرورياً، والعلم المطلق جزء منه، فيتم التقريب فليس بشيء لأن معنى ذلك أن العلم بحصول العلم بعد الالتفات ضروري لا أن تصوره ضروري حتى يلزم ضرورية المطلق. لأنه قوله : ( والعلم أحد تصوري هذا التصديق) إنما قال : أحد التصورين من غير تعيين يجوز أن يجعل موضوعاً بأن يقال : العلم بهذا العلم ضروري، وأن يجعل محمولاً بأن يقال : كل أحد عالم بهذا العلم بالضرورة، وبهذا ظهر أنه لا يجوز أن يقال أحد التصورات الثلاثة، فمن قال : إنما لم يقل أحد التصورات اتباعاً للمتقدمين، وإن اعتبار تصور النسبة في التصديق من تدقيقات المتأخرين، فقد ركب شططاً .
It may not be said: We do not restrict to what was mentioned, but we say: Every person knows necessarily that he exists, and also knows likewise that he knows that, and knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations (taṣawwur) of this assent (taṣdīq), and it also [is a conceptualization]; so its conceptualization preceding the intuitive assent is more fitting to be intuitive. We [respond] intuitive. His saying: So its occurrence is not identical to its conceptualization. And what has been said that knowledge of knowledge after attention is necessary as the author stated in al-Aʿrāḍ, so knowledge of this knowledge is necessary, and absolute knowledge is part of it, so the argument is complete—it is nothing, because the meaning of that is that knowledge of the occurrence of knowledge after attention is necessary, not that its conceptualization is necessary such that the necessity of the absolute follows. Because his saying: (And knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations of this assent) he only said one of the two conceptualizations without specification, so it is permissible to make it subject, saying: knowledge of this knowledge is necessary, or to make it predicate, saying: everyone knows this knowledge necessarily. And by this it appears that it is not permissible to say one of the three conceptualizations. So whoever said that he did not say one of the conceptualizations following the earlier scholars, and that considering the conceptualization of the relation in assent is from the investigations of the later scholars—he has gone far astray.

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objector argues that since everyone knows that they exist, they also know that they know this—so knowledge is a component of that assent, and thus its conceptualization (being prior to the intuitive assent) should be intuitive as well. The answer first notes that the occurrence of knowledge is not its conceptualization; the claim that knowledge of knowledge after attention is necessary refers to the occurrence, not the conceptualization. Then it points out that the phrase 'knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations' does not specify which part (subject or predicate) it is, so it cannot be used to argue that the conceptualization of absolute knowledge is necessary. This refutes the attempt to force a regress.

Key Terms
تصديق
تصوران
بديهي
علم بالعلم
التفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If knowledge of knowledge is necessary, then the conceptualization of that knowledge (and absolute knowledge) is also necessary.
Correction: The necessity applies to the occurrence of knowledge after attention, not to its conceptualization. The two are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the objection that knowledge is a component of an intuitive assent fail?
A) Because the objector denies intuitive knowledge.
B) Because the occurrence of knowledge is not identical to its conceptualization, and the phrase 'one of the two' is unspecified.
C) Because the author does not accept that everyone knows their existence.
D) Because the objector relies on later scholars' terminology.

Key Takeaway
Necessity of the occurrence of knowledge does not entail necessity of its conceptualization, and an ambiguous term cannot anchor a proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows how an ambiguous phrase can derail an entire argument. Where in your own understanding of Islam have you built beliefs on terms you haven't carefully defined? Will you take time to clarify them?
?
The objector confused necessary occurrence with necessary conceptualization. Is there any area of your life—belief, practice, or relationship—where you have claimed to 'know' something without truly conceptualizing it? What will you do to move from mere occurrence to genuine understanding?

Intuitive Assent Does Not Necessitate Intuitive Conceptualizations
Author refutes the claim that an intuitive assent implies its components are intuitive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intuitiveness of an assent does not require its conceptual components to be intuitive, and how the author defends this position against objections.

Core Idea
Intuitive assent can exist even if its component concepts are acquired.

This passage presents a critical refinement in Islamic epistemology: the distinction between the intuitiveness of a judgment and the intuitiveness of its conceptual components. The author refutes the claim that an intuitive assent must have intuitive parts, using the example of children and simpletons to show that assent can be intuitive without any acquisition. Let's read the source to see how he dismantles the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the objection, response, and further clarifications.
The passage includes many parenthetical glosses; focus on the main argument flow.
2
Identify the main objection
The objector claims that an intuitive assent could have all acquired conceptualizations, so intuitiveness of assent does not imply intuitiveness of its components.
This objection is presented as 'فإن قلت'.
3
Follow the refutation
The author responds by stating that this particular assent is free from reflection entirely, not just in judgment but also in its ends, as even simpletons have it.
The author uses the example of simple-minded and children to show the assent is intuitive without any acquisition.
4
Understand the terminological dispute
The author dismisses the dispute about whether assent is solely the judgment or includes conceptualizations, because the assent under discussion is absolutely free from reflection.
This shows that the intuition of the assent is independent of the definitional debate.
5
Address the circularity concern
A potential circularity is resolved: the claim is for the intuitiveness of a specific part (knowledge) in detail, while the evidence is the intuitiveness of the whole assent in summary, so no circularity.
The author mentions an analog in the discussion of existence.

الأول، لأنه وقع فيه العلم الخاص، أعني العلم بكونه موجوداً موضوعاً، وحكم عليه بأنه ضروري ليمكن أن يقال : لا يلزم من كون العلم بأنه موجود ضرورياً بداهة العلم الذي وقع فيه موضوعاً، لأنه تصديق ضروري ولا For if you say in answer to this exposition: from the intuitiveness (badāhah) of the assent, the intuitiveness of its two conceptualizations does not follow, nor the intuitiveness of anything from them; for the intuitive assent is that which does not depend, after conceptualization of the two ends, on reflection. So it is possible that all its conceptualizations are acquired (kasbiyya), and thus the inference from the intuitiveness of the assent to the intuitiveness of any of its conceptualizations is not valid at all. I say in refutation of this answer: the claim is the occurrence of this assent without reflection in the judgment nor in anything from its ends, for the simple-minded and children are not free from it—those who cannot engage in acquisition, neither in judgment nor in conceptualization. And the dispute about terminology—that assent is only the judgment alone and the conceptualizations of its ends are conditions external to it, so the intuitive part is the judgment independent of inference even if its conceptualizations are theoretical, and assent is not an expression of the composite whole consisting of the judgment and the conceptualizations of its ends such that its intuitiveness would necessitate the intuitiveness of its conceptualizations—does not avail in this context, because you know that this assent under discussion is absolutely free from reflection. Then he began a response to 'it may not be said' with his saying: 'For we say: it suffices in assent the conceptualization of the two ends in some manner, and there is no need to conceptualize them by essence (bi-al-kunn),' just as we judge about a specific body seen from a distance that it occupies a specific place, while being ignorant of its true nature—whether it is a human or a stone—indeed even while being ignorant of the true nature of space. His saying: (In answer to this exposition) he specified it with this exposition, although it is valid as an answer to the first exposition because it intervened between the second exposition and its answer 'for we say' etc. It is valid as an answer to the first exposition only because in it occurred the particular knowledge, namely the knowledge that he exists as subject, and it was ruled necessary so that it could be said: from the necessity of the knowledge that he exists, the intuitiveness of the knowledge that occurred as subject does not follow, because it is a necessary assent and from its intuitiveness the intuitiveness of its ends does not follow—so reflect. His saying: (For the simple-minded are not free etc.) He pointed by this proof to the fact that the proponent of the intuitiveness of the assent disregards the specificity of the ends, and the proponent of the intuitiveness of the assent [as proof] for the intuitiveness of the knowledge that is one of its ends in particular—so there is no circularity. His saying: (Absolutely free from reflection) i.e., with respect to the judgment and the two ends, whether they are parts or conditions. His saying: (Nor) in anything of its ends—it may not be said: then there follows circularity because one of its ends is the knowledge whose conceptualization is intended to be proven intuitive; for we say: the claimant claims the intuitiveness of a specific part in detail, namely knowledge, and the evidence is the intuitiveness of this assent with all its parts in sum, so there is no circularity due to the difference of aspect. Its analog will come in the discussion of existence with its conclusion. His saying: (And there is no need to conceptualize them by essence) and even if it is granted, it would only hold if the universal were essential (dhātī) to the particular knowledge as the commentator mentioned earlier. Yes, if one argued that everyone knows that he has absolute knowledge, then the author's answer would be determined.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of an objection that the intuitiveness of an assent does not imply the intuitiveness of its conceptual components. The author first presents the objection: an intuitive assent is defined as not depending on reflection after conceptualizing the two ends, so its conceptualizations could all be acquired; thus one cannot infer intuitiveness of any conceptualization from the intuitiveness of the assent. The response argues that the claim is that this specific assent occurs without any reflection at all, not just in judgment but also in the ends, as even simpletons and children have it while incapable of acquisition. The terminological dispute about assent being solely the judgment, with conceptualizations as external conditions, is dismissed because this assent is free from reflection absolutely. Then a further response addresses a potential objection about circularity: the claim is for the intuitiveness of a specific part (knowledge) in detail, while the evidence is the intuitiveness of the whole assent in summary, so no circularity. Lastly, it notes that even if one concedes that conceptualization by essence is not needed, the argument for absolute knowledge would only hold if the universal were essential to the particular, which is not granted. An alternative proof is mentioned.

Self-Evidence of Knowledge: Objection and Answer
Sanusi defends self-evidence of assent with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the self-evidence of assent against objections that it requires self-evident conceptual components.

Core Idea
Self-evident assent needs only general conceptualization of its terms, not their full essence.

Sanusi now addresses a crucial objection: if assent is self-evident, does that require its concepts to be self-evident too? He argues that only a general awareness of terms is needed, as shown by examples from everyday judgment. This lesson walks through his refutation and clarifies the nature of self-evident knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Notice how the opponent distinguishes between assent and its conceptualizations, arguing that self-evidence of assent does not imply self-evidence of its concepts.
The opponent allows that concepts may be acquired (kashfi).
2
Follow Sanusi's first answer
He appeals to the fact that even imbeciles and children have this assent without any acquisition, showing it is independent of reasoning in both judgment and concepts.
3
Examine the further objection and answer
The opponent insists that assent requires essence-conceptualization; Sanusi shows that a general conceptualization suffices, using examples like judging a distant body.
Examples: judging a body to occupy space without knowing its essence.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that self-evidence applies to the judgment itself, not necessarily to the concepts in their essence, clarifying the scope of self-evident knowledge.
The passage concludes that conceptualization of absolute knowledge in a general way is self-evident, but not in essence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - في تعريف مطلق العلم
فإن قلت في جواب هذا التقرير: لا يلزم من بداهة التصديق بداهة تصوريه، ولا بداهة شيء منهما، فإن التصديق البديهي ما لا يتوقف بعد تصور الطرفين على نظر، فجاز أن تكون تصوراته بأسرها كسبية فلا يصح الاستدلال ببداهة التصديق على بداهة شيء من تصوراته أصلاً.
قلت: في رد هذا الجواب أن المدعي حصول هذا التصديق بلا نظر في الحكم ولا في شيء من أطرافه، إذ لا تخلو عنه البله والصبيان الذين لا يتأتى منهم الاكتساب لا في حكم ولا في تصور. والنزاع في التسمية بأن التصديق إنما هو الحكم وحده وتصورات أطرافه شروط له خارج عنه، فالبديهي منه هو الحكم المستغني عن الاستدلال وإن كانت تصوراته نظرية، وليس التصديق عبارة عن المجموع المركب من الحكم وتصورات أطرافه حتى تكون بداهته مستلزمة لبداهة تصوراته لا يجدي طائلاً في هذا المقام لما عرفت من أن هذا التصديق الذي نحن فيه مستغن عن النظر مطلقاً.
ثم شرع في جواب لا يقال: بقوله: لأنا نقول يكفي في التصديق تصور الطرفين بوجه ما، ولا يحتاج فيه إلى تصورهما بالكنه، كما نحكم على جسم معين مشاهد من بعيد بأنه شاغل لحيز معين، مع الجهل بحقيقته هل إنسان أو حجر، بل ومع الجهل بحقيقة الحيز والشغل. بل نحكم بأن الواجب تعالى إما نفس أو لا، وإن لم نعلم حقيقتهما بكنههما، بل باعتبار أمر عام عارض لهما، ككونه صانعاً للعالم وكونه مدبرة للبدن مثلاً. فاللازم مما ذكرتم أن يكون تصور مطلق العلم بوجه ما بديهياً، ولا نزاع فيه، بل في تصوره بحسب الحقيقة.
The Second Observatory - On Defining Absolute Knowledge. For if you say in answer to this statement: The self-evidence of assent does not entail the self-evidence of its conceptualization, nor the self-evidence of anything of them; for self-evident assent is that which, after conceptualizing the two ends, does not depend on reasoning, so it is possible that all its conceptualizations are acquired, and thus it is not valid to infer from the self-evidence of assent the self-evidence of any of its conceptualizations at all. I say: In refuting this answer, the claimant asserts that this assent occurs without reasoning in the judgment nor in any of its parts, since imbeciles and children, who cannot engage in acquisition either in judgment or in conceptualization, are not free from it. The dispute about terminology—that assent is merely the judgment alone and conceptualizations of its ends are conditions external to it, so the self-evident part is the judgment independent of inference even if its conceptualizations are theoretical, and assent is not a composite whole of the judgment and conceptualizations of its ends such that its self-evidence would entail the self-evidence of its conceptualizations—is of no avail in this context, because you know that this assent of which we speak is absolutely independent of reasoning. Then he proceeded to answer the statement 'it may not be said' with his words: For we say that in assent a conceptualization of the two ends in some manner suffices, and there is no need to conceptualize them in essence, just as we judge of a particular body seen from afar that it occupies a particular space, while being ignorant of its essence—whether it is man or stone—and even while being ignorant of the essence of space and occupation. Rather, we judge of the Necessary Exalted that He is either a soul or not, even though we do not know their essences, but by some general property incidental to them, such as His being the Maker of the world and His being the manager of the body, for example. So the necessary consequence of what you have mentioned is that the conceptualization of absolute knowledge in some manner is self-evident, and there is no dispute about that, but rather about its conceptualization according to its essence.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument concerning the self-evidence of knowledge. The objection states that the self-evidence of an assent (تصديق) does not imply the self-evidence of its conceptual components (تصورات), because self-evident assent only requires that the judgment itself be immediate after conceptualizing its terms, while those terms themselves could be acquired. The answer refutes this by pointing out that the assent in question is found without any reasoning in either the judgment or its parts, as even imbeciles and children possess it, who cannot acquire anything. The objection that assent is only the judgment and its conceptualizations are external conditions is deemed irrelevant because the assent at issue is absolutely independent of reasoning. Then a further objection (لا يقال) is anticipated: that conceptualization of the terms in essence is needed. The answer explains that a general conceptualization suffices, illustrated by examples: judging a distant body to occupy space without knowing its essence, or judging the Necessary Existent to be a soul or not without knowing their essences. The conclusion is that the conceptualization of absolute knowledge in a general way is self-evident, but its conceptualization in essence is not.

تصور
بديهي
كسبي
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a judgment is self-evident, then all its conceptual components must also be self-evident.
Correction: Only a general conceptualization of the terms is needed for the self-evident judgment, not a full essence-conceptualization.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what suffices for a self-evident assent regarding its conceptual components?
A) Conceptualization of the essence (كنه) of both terms
B) Conceptualization of the terms in some general manner (بوجه ما)
C) No conceptualization at all
D) Acquired reasoning about the terms

Key Takeaway
Self-evident assent requires only a general conceptualization of its terms, not a full essence-knowledge.

Defining Knowledge Without Circularity: Sanusi's Resolution
Sanusi argues that knowledge cannot be defined without circularity and resolves the objection by distinguishing occurrence from conceptualization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi resolves the apparent circularity in defining knowledge by distinguishing between the occurrence of particular knowledge and conceptualization of absolute knowledge.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge by something else requires only a particular instance, not full conceptualization.

Sanusi presents a powerful objection against defining knowledge: any definition either relies on itself or on something else known through knowledge, leading to circularity. He then offers a nuanced resolution that distinguishes two different cognitive operations. This passage is crucial for understanding how Islamic theology handles foundational definitions without infinite regress or circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and its English translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice the structure: objection, qualification, answer, and resolution.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that if knowledge is acquired and definable, its definition would be either by itself (impossible) or by something else, but that something else is known by knowledge, leading to circularity.
3
Understand the qualification
The objection is only valid against those who claim knowledge is known in essence but not necessarily. Others might say the essence is unknowable.
This limits the scope of the argument.
4
Study the resolution
Sanusi answers by distinguishing between the occurrence (حصول) of a particular knowledge and the conceptualization (تصور) of absolute knowledge. The other thing is known by a particular instance, not by conceptualizing the essence of knowledge. Thus, when defining knowledge, we seek conceptualization of its essence, which depends on a particular knowledge of the other thing and on the essence occurring within that particular. That occurrence does not depend on conceptualization, so no circularity.
The key is the one-way dependence.
5
Grasp the core insight
Recognize that the failure to distinguish occurrence from conceptualization is the source of the ambiguity. This insight resolves both the objection and a related ambiguity.
This is the heart of Sanusi's contribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني أن العلم لو كان كسبياً معرفاً، فإما أن يتعرف بنفسه، وهو باطل قطعاً، أو بغيره، وهو أيضاً باطل لأن غير العلم إنما يعلم بالعلم، فلو علم العلم بغيره لزم الدور لتوقف معلوميه كل منهما في معلومية الآخر حينئذ.
وهذا الوجه على تقدير صحته حجة على من يقول: إنه أي مطلق العلم معلوم بحسب حقيقته لكن لا بالضرورة، فإنه إذ لم يسلم كونه معلوماً كذلك اتجه أن يقال: لا يلزم من امتناع كونه مكتسباً أن يكون ضرورياً، لجواز أن يكون تصوره بكنهه ممتنعاً.
والجواب: أن غير العلم إنما يعلم بحصول علم جزئي متعلق به لا بتصور حقيقة العلم المطلق، فإن أكثر الناس يعلمون أشياء كثيرة، وليسوا يتصورون حقيقة العلم المطلق. والذي نحاول أن نعلمه أي نطلب أن نحصله على ذلك التقدير بغير العلم تصور حقيقة العلم فلا دور، إذ اللازم أن يكون تصور حقيقة العلم موقوفاً على حصول علم جزئي متعلق بذلك الغير وعلى حصول حقيقة العلم في ضمن ذلك الجزئي أيضاً، فيتوقف تصور حقيقته على حصولها في ضمن بعض جزئياتها، وليس ذلك الحصول متوقفاً على تصور حقيقته فلا دور.
وحاصل حل الشبهتين بالفرق بين حصول العلم المطلق بنفسه في الذهن و بين تصوره، وذلك لأن منشأهما عدم الفرق بينهما، ففي الشبة الأولى تخيل أنه إذا حصل بالضرورة علم جزئي قائم بالنفس كانت ماهية العلم حاصلة بالضرورة في ضمنه قائمة بالنفس أيضاً.
The second aspect: if knowledge were acquired and definable, then either it is defined by itself, which is absolutely false, or by something else, which is also false, because something other than knowledge is only known by knowledge, so if knowledge were known by something else, circularity would result, for the knownness of each of the two would depend on the knownness of the other. This aspect, if valid, is an argument against one who says that absolute knowledge is known according to its essence but not necessarily, for if he does not grant that it is known in that way, it follows that we can say: the impossibility of its being acquired does not entail that it is necessary, for it is possible that its conceptualization in essence is impossible. The answer: that something other than knowledge is known by the occurrence of a particular knowledge related to it, not by conceptualizing the essence of absolute knowledge; for most people know many things without conceptualizing the essence of absolute knowledge. What we attempt to know—i.e., we seek to obtain on that supposition—by something other than knowledge is the conceptualization of the essence of knowledge, so there is no circularity, since the required implication is that the conceptualization of the essence of knowledge depends on the occurrence of a particular knowledge related to that other and on the occurrence of the essence of knowledge within that particular as well. Thus the conceptualization of its essence depends on its occurrence within some of its particulars, and that occurrence is not dependent on the conceptualization of its essence, so there is no circularity. The core of resolving the two ambiguities is the distinction between the occurrence of absolute knowledge itself in the mind and its conceptualization, because their source is the failure to distinguish between them. In the first ambiguity, it was imagined that if a particular knowledge subsisting in the soul occurs necessarily, then the essence of knowledge occurs necessarily within it, also subsisting in the soul.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument and its resolution concerning the definition of knowledge. It begins with a proof: if knowledge were acquired and definable, its definition would either be by itself (circular) or by something else, but that something else is only known through knowledge, leading to circularity. This argument is then qualified: it only refutes those who claim knowledge is known in essence but not necessarily, because if one does not grant that, it could be that the essence of knowledge is inconceivable. The answer to the resulting objection distinguishes between the occurrence (حصول) of particular knowledge and the conceptualization (تصور) of the essence of absolute knowledge. The other thing is known by a particular instance of knowledge, not by conceptualizing absolute knowledge itself. When we attempt to define knowledge by something else, we seek to conceptualize its essence, but this conceptualization depends on the occurrence of a particular knowledge of that other thing and on the occurrence of the essence of knowledge within that particular. This is not circular because the occurrence of the essence does not depend on its conceptualization. The resolution of both ambiguities lies in distinguishing the occurrence of absolute knowledge in the mind from its conceptualization; the failure to make this distinction generated the doubts.

Correction: The passage shows that only a particular, non-conceptual knowledge of the other thing is necessary; conceptualization of absolute knowledge is only sought in the definition itself, and that conceptualization depends on a particular instance of knowledge, not vice versa.

The First Shubha on Defining Absolute Knowledge: Circularity and Its Resolution
Presents and resolves the first doubt on defining absolute knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining absolute knowledge does not lead to circularity, and the key distinction between the occurrence of knowledge (husul) and its conceptualization (tasawwur).

Core Idea
The occurrence of knowledge is not the same as conceptualizing its essence.

This passage presents the first major doubt against defining absolute knowledge: the charge of circularity. If knowledge can only be known through something else, that something else must already be known, leading to an infinite regress or loop. The answer lies in distinguishing between the mere occurrence of knowledge in the mind and the full conceptualization of its essence. This distinction resolves the doubt and clarifies the nature of knowledge definition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the shubha
Read the passage and identify the circularity argument: knowledge cannot be known by itself, nor by something else without circularity, because that something else is known only through knowledge.
The shubha uses a two-option argument: self-knowledge (impossible) or other-knowledge (circular).
2
Examine the objection and answer
The opponent may claim that even if knowledge is acquired, it could be impossible to conceptualize its essence. The answer explains that other things are known through particular knowledge, not through conceptualizing absolute knowledge.
The key point: we can know many things without conceptualizing the essence of knowledge.
3
Grasp the resolution
The resolution distinguishes between husul (occurrence) and tasawwur (conceptualization). The first shubha mistakenly equated the occurrence of a particular knowledge with conceptualization of absolute knowledge.
Occurrence is automatic when a mental image arises; conceptualization requires grasping the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - في تعريف مطلق العلم
والشغل ( بل نحكم بأن الواجب ( تعالى إما) نفس أو لا وإن لم نعلم حقيقتهما يكنههما بل باعتبار أمر (عام) عارض لهما، ككونه صانعاً للعالم، وكونه مدبرة للبدن مثلاً، فاللازم مما ذكرتم أن يكون تصور مطلق العلم بوجه ما بديهياً، ولا نزاع فيه بل في تصوره بحسب الحقيقة الوجه ( الثاني أن ( العلم لو كان كسبياً معرفاً فإما أن يتعرف بنفسه، وهو باطل قطعاً، أو بغيره وهو أيضاً باطل لأن ( غير العلم إنما يعلم بالعلم فلو علم العلم بغيره لزم الدور) لتوقف معلوميه كل منهما في معلومية الآخر حينئذ (وهذا الوجه على تقدير صحته ) حجةٌ على من يقول : إنه ) أي مطلق العلم ( معلوم ) بحسب حقيقته لكن ( لا بالضرورة ) فإنه إذ لم يسلم كونه معلوماً، كذلك اتجه أن يقال : لا يلزم من امتناع كونه مكتسباً أن يكون ضرورياً، لجواز أن يكون تصور بكنهه ممتنعاً ) والجواب أن غير العلم إنما يعلم بحصول علم جزئي ) متعلق به ( لا بتصور حقيقة العلم المطلق فإن أكثر الناس يعلمون أشياء كثيرة، وليسوا يتصورون حقيقة العلم المطلق ( والذي نحاول أن نعلمه ( أي نطلب أن نحصله على ذلك التقدير ( بغير العلم تصور حقيقة العلم فلا (دور) إذ اللازم أن يكون تصور حقيقة العلم موقوفاً على حصول علم جزئي متعلق بذلك الغير وعلى حصول حقيقة العلم في ضمن ذلك الجزئي أيضاً، فيتوقف تصور حقيقته على حصولها في ضمن بعض جزئياتها، وليس ذلك الحصول متوقفاً على تصور حقيقته فلا دور ( وحاصل حل الشبهتين بالفرق بين حصول العلم المطلق بنفسه في الذهن (و) بين (تصوره )، وذلك لأن منشأهما عدم الفرق بينهما ففي الشبة الأولى تخيل أنه إذا حصل بالضرورة علم جزئي قائم بالنفس كانت ماهية العلم حاصلة بالضرورة في ضمنه قائمة بالنفس أيضاً، وهذا معنى كون تلك الماهية متصورة
The Second Observant Point - On the Definition of Absolute Knowledge. And the occupation (but we judge that the obligatory (exalted be He) either is the self or not, even if we do not know their quiddities as they really are, but by virtue of a general accident that occurs to them, such as His being the Maker of the world and His being the Manager of the body, for example. So the necessary consequence of what you mentioned is that the conceptualization of absolute knowledge in some respect is self-evident, and there is no dispute about that, but rather about its conceptualization according to reality. The second aspect is that if knowledge were acquired and definable, then either it would be known by itself, which is absolutely false, or by something else, which is also false, because other than knowledge is only known by knowledge; so if knowledge were known by something else, circularity would occur, due to the dependence of each of the two known things on the other in being known at that time. And this aspect, if sound, is an argument against those who say that it, i.e., absolute knowledge, is known according to its reality but not necessarily. For if he does not concede that it is known, then it may be said that the impossibility of its being acquired does not necessitate that it be necessary, because it is possible that conceptualization of its essence is impossible. And the answer is that other than knowledge is only known by the occurrence of a particular knowledge related to it, not by conceptualization of the reality of absolute knowledge; for most people know many things but do not conceptualize the reality of absolute knowledge. And what we attempt to know—i.e., we seek to obtain it on that estimation—by something other than knowledge is the conceptualization of the reality of knowledge, so there is no circularity, because the necessary consequence is that the conceptualization of the reality of knowledge depends on the occurrence of a particular knowledge related to that other, and also on the occurrence of the reality of knowledge within that particular. So the conceptualization of its reality depends on its occurrence within some of its particulars, and that occurrence is not dependent on the conceptualization of its reality, so there is no circularity. And the summary of the solution of the two shubhat is by distinguishing between the occurrence of absolute knowledge itself in the mind and its conceptualization. This is because their source is the failure to distinguish between them. So in the first shubha, he imagined that if a particular knowledge subsisting in the soul necessarily occurs, then the quiddity of knowledge necessarily occurs within it, also subsisting in the soul, and this is the meaning of that quiddity being conceptualized.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents and resolves the first shubha concerning the definition of absolute knowledge. It begins by stating the second observational point: defining absolute knowledge. The text then takes up an objection about circularity. The argument is that if knowledge were acquired (kashiyan) and definable, it would have to be known either by itself (impossible) or by something else. The latter leads to circularity because anything other than knowledge is only known through knowledge, so knowing knowledge through something else would require that knowledge already be known. The opponent might argue that the impossibility of knowledge being acquired does not make it self-evident (daruri), as it could be impossible to conceive its essence. The answer given is that other things are known through the occurrence of a particular knowledge of them, not through conceptualizing absolute knowledge. Most people know many things without conceptualizing absolute knowledge. When we seek to define knowledge, we are aiming for its conceptualization via something other than knowledge, but this does not create circularity because the conceptualization of absolute knowledge depends on the occurrence of both a particular knowledge of that other thing and the occurrence of the reality of knowledge within that particular. The occurrence does not depend on the conceptualization, so no loop occurs. The resolution of both shubhat lies in distinguishing between the occurrence (husul) of absolute knowledge itself in the mind and its conceptualization (tasawwur). The first shubha mistakenly assumed that when a particular knowledge necessarily occurs in the soul, the quiddity of knowledge also necessarily occurs within it, equating occurrence with conceptualization. The text clarifies that this is only the occurrence, not conceptualization.

Correction: Occurrence (husul) is the presence of a mental image, while conceptualization (tasawwur) is grasping the essence. Most people have particular knowledge without conceptualizing the essence of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Defining absolute knowledge does not lead to circularity because we can have particular knowledge without conceptualizing the essence of knowledge; the key is distinguishing occurrence from conceptualization.

Distinguishing Occurrence from Conceptualization of Knowledge
Sanusi resolves two shubhas by distinguishing occurrence from conceptualization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the mental occurrence (حصول) of knowledge and its conceptualization (تصور), and how this distinction resolves the circularity argument.

Core Idea
Knowledge can be present in the mind without being conceptualized.

The author addresses a key confusion in epistemology: the difference between having knowledge in the mind and being aware of that knowledge. He introduces two modes of mental impression—occurrence by essence and occurrence by likeness—to resolve apparent circularity. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding how definitions of knowledge work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two shubhas mentioned and the resolution.
The passage uses the analogy of courage to illustrate the distinction.
2
Identify the two modes of impression
The passage describes two ways knowledge is impressed in the soul: by itself (occurrence) and by its likeness (conceptualization). Find these and understand the difference.
3
Analyze the courage analogy
The analogy of courage shows that having courage (occurrence) does not require conceptualizing courage. Similarly, conceptualizing courage does not make one courageous. Reflect on this.
This analogy is key to grasping the distinction.
4
See how the shubhas collapse
Understand that once the distinction is clear, the two shubhas (circularity and other) are resolved. The passage states this explicitly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا معنى كون تلك الماهية متصورة وفي الشبهة الثانية تخيل أن تصور ماهية العلم إذا توقف على حصول علم جزئي متعلق بالغير، ولا شك أنه يتوقف على حصول ماهيته في ضمنه قائمة في الذهن وهذا معنى تصورها فقد توقف كل منهما على الآخر، وإذا ظهر الفرق بينهما بأن ارتسام ماهية العلم في النفس على وجهين، أحدهما : أن ترتسم فيها بنفسها في ضمن جزئياتها، وذلك حصولها وليس تصورها ولا مستلزماً على قياس حصول الشجاعة للنفس الموجب لاتصافها بها، من غير أن تتصورها والثاني : أن ترتسم فيها بمثالها، وبصورتها وهذا هو تصورها لا حصولها على قياس تصور الشجاعة الذي لا يوجب اتصاف النفس بها، وهو المطلوب بتعريفها اضمحلت الشبهتان بالكلية .
[ المذهب الثاني ] : وبه قال إمام الحرمين والغزالي : ( أنه ليس ضرورياً) بل هو نظري (و) لكن ( يعسر تحديده وربما نصرا بالدليل الثاني ) إنما قال: ربما لأن
قوله: (فقد توقف إلخ) أي يكون تصور ماهية العلم موقوفاً على حصول العلم الجزئي المتعلق بغيره، ويكون ذلك الحصول موقوفاً أيضاً على تصور ماهيته وهو الدور. قوله : ( وإذا ظهر الفرق إلخ بين الفرق بين الحصول الاتصافي وبين العلم الارتسامي الذي هو حصول الشيء بصورته بانفكاك كل منهما عن الآخر، ولم يتعرض للفرق بينه وبين العلم الحضوري بأن حصول الشيء على وجه الاتصاف لا يستلزم الالتفات إليه لأن الكلام في بيان المغايرة بين حصول العلم المطلق، وبين تصوره ولا شك أن العلم المطلق أمر خارج عن الذهن، لیس نفسه ولا من صفاته فالعلم به لا يكون إلا ارتسامياً.
قوله: (لا حصولها أي ارتسامها من حيث إنها ارتسام بصورتها، لا يكون حصول نفسها، وإن كان من حيث أن تلك الصورة فرد من العلم المطلق حصول نفسها في ضمن ذلك الفرد .
قوله : ( وذلك حصولها وليس (تصورها أي المراد بالتصور في هذا المقام هو الارتسام الكلي أعني العلم الحصولي لأنه المتنازع فيه بالضرورية والنظرية فإطلاق التصور على نفس حصولها بمعنى آخر على ما قالوا من أن تصور الصفات النفسية يكون بحصول حقائقها في : النفس لا يقدح فيما ذكره كما لا يخفى .
.
قوله : ( وهذا) هو تصورها لا (حصولها فإن قلت تصورها فرد من أفرادها وجزئي من جزئياتها ففي تصور ماهية العلم حصولها في ضمن فرد من أفرادها كما في الأول، فما معنى قوله : لا حصولها قلت معناه أن النفس لا توصف بالعلم باعتبار ارتسامه فيها بمثله، وإنما توصف باعتبار ارتسامه فيها بنفسه، ولو في ضمن هذا الارتسام الثاني، وهذا حق لا شبهة فيه .
And this is the meaning of that essence being conceptualized. And in the second shubha, it is imagined that the conceptualization of the essence of knowledge, if it depends on the occurrence of a particular knowledge related to another, and there is no doubt that it depends on the occurrence of its essence within it, existing in the mind, and this is the meaning of its conceptualization, then each of them depends on the other. And when the distinction between them appears—namely, that the impression of the essence of knowledge in the soul is in two ways: one, that it is impressed in it by itself, within its particulars, and that is its occurrence (حصول), not its conceptualization, and it does not necessitate [conceptualization]; analogously to the occurrence of courage in the soul which necessitates its being described by it, without its being conceptualized; and the second, that it is impressed in it by its likeness and its form, and this is its conceptualization, not its occurrence, analogously to the conceptualization of courage which does not necessitate the soul's being described by it—and that is what is sought by its definition, the two shubhas collapse entirely.
[The Second School]: And according to it, Imam al-Haramayn and al-Ghazali said: (It is not necessary) but rather theoretical, and (but) it is difficult to define, and perhaps they supported it by the second proof. He said: 'perhaps' because the support by it is illusory. 
His saying: ('For it depends, etc.') meaning that the conceptualization of the essence of knowledge is dependent on the occurrence of particular knowledge related to another, and that occurrence is also dependent on the conceptualization of its essence—which is circularity. His saying: ('And when the distinction appears, etc.') concerning the distinction between occurrent attribution and impressionist knowledge, which is the occurrence of a thing by its form, each being separate from the other; and he did not address the distinction between it and presential knowledge, because the occurrence of a thing in the mode of attribution does not entail attention to it, because the discourse is on explaining the difference between the occurrence of absolute knowledge and its conceptualization; and there is no doubt that absolute knowledge is something external to the mind, not itself nor one of its attributes, so knowledge of it can only be impressionist. 
His saying: ('Not its occurrence') meaning its impression, inasmuch as it is an impression by its form, is not the occurrence of itself, even if inasmuch as that form is an individual of absolute knowledge, [it is] the occurrence of itself within that individual. 
His saying: ('And that is its occurrence and not its conceptualization') meaning that what is meant by conceptualization in this context is universal impression, i.e., acquisitive knowledge (العلم الحصولي), because it is the point of contention regarding necessity and theory; so applying conceptualization to its very occurrence in another sense—as they said that the conceptualization of psychic attributes occurs by the occurrence of their realities in the soul—does not harm what he mentioned, as is not hidden. 
His saying: ('And this is its conceptualization, not its occurrence') for if you say: its conceptualization is an individual of its individuals and a particular of its particulars, so in the conceptualization of the essence of knowledge there is its occurrence within an individual of its individuals, as in the first; so what is the meaning of his saying 'not its occurrence'? I say: its meaning is that the soul is not described as knowledge by virtue of its impression in it by its likeness, but rather it is described by virtue of its impression in it by itself, even if within this second impression; and this is true, no doubt about it.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a combined exposition: it presents the main argument distinguishing between mental occurrence (حصول) and conceptualization (تصور) of knowledge, introduces the second opinion of Imam al-Haramayn and al-Ghazali, and then provides detailed commentary on the distinction. The author begins by stating the meaning of an essence being conceptualized and then describes the second shubha: a circular dependency between the conceptualization of the essence of knowledge and the occurrence of a particular knowledge. He resolves the circularity by showing that the impression of knowledge in the soul occurs in two ways: (1) the essence itself being present in the soul (its occurrence), which does not entail conceptualization—like the presence of courage in the soul, which makes the soul courageous without the soul conceptualizing courage; (2) the essence being represented by its likeness (conceptualization), which does not entail the attribute's presence—like conceptualizing courage without being courageous. This distinction collapses both shubhas. The text then transitions to the second school (المذهب الثاني), stating that knowledge is not self-evident but theoretical and difficult to define, supported by a second proof, though the support is considered illusory. The subsequent commentary explains the circularity argument, elaborates on the difference between attributive occurrence and impressionist knowledge, clarifies that absolute knowledge is external to the mind, and addresses potential objections about the nature of conceptualization and its relationship to occurrence. The commentary ends by affirming that the soul is described as knowledge only by the impression of knowledge itself, not by its likeness, even if that impression occurs within a second-order impression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that having knowledge in the mind is the same as conceptualizing it.
Correction: The passage distinguishes: occurrence is presence without awareness, conceptualization is awareness without presence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the occurrence of courage in the soul illustrate?
A) That conceptualization requires occurrence.
B) That occurrence does not require conceptualization.
C) That courage is always conceptualized.
D) That occurrence and conceptualization are identical.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge can exist in the mind in two different ways: as an attribute (occurrence) or as an object of thought (conceptualization), and confusing them leads to logical circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Was it through its occurrence in your actions (without conceptualizing it) or through conceptualizing it later? How does this distinction change your understanding of self-knowledge? ?

Have you ever satisfied yourself with a textbook answer while your soul lacks the actual attribute of that knowledge? Name one Islamic concept you have conceptualized but not yet allowed to occur in your soul. Pray for Allah to grant you its true occurrence.
